No one\nwas severely hurt.\n\"There were devices made out of ampules containing a so far unknown\nsubstance, which on dispersal gave out a strong pungent smell like\ngarlic,\" said a spokeswoman for the local Federal Security Service,\nthe successor of the Soviet-era KGB.\nShe said the service was struggling to identify the gas released in\nthe shop. Frankel <Richard.Frankel@med.va.gov>\n\n\nHansen's disease is the preferred terminology in the USA.\n\nThe national figures have shown fewer cases in recent years. [CDC's]MMWR\nreported 93 cases in 2003 and 82 cases in 2004. The disease is not\nreportable in all states, so there may be a few more.\n\nIt is hard to understand why 7 to 10 days are needed to confirm the\ndiagnosis. While the disease is often misdiagnosed, once it is\nsuspected, a competent dermatopathologist should be able to confirm\nthe diagnosis.\n\nIsolation is generally not used for patients with Hansen's disease in\nthe USA. While there are differences of opinion with regard to the\ndegree of contagiousness, most people have natural immunity, and the\ninstitution of antimicrobial therapy quickly reduces the risk of\ntransmission.\n\n--\nRichard I. Frankel, MD, MPH\nVA Pacific Islands Health Care System\nProfessor Emeritus, University of Hawaii\nHonolulu, HI\n<Richard.Frankel@med.va.gov>\n\n[ProMED thanks Dr. Frankel for his comments.\n\nWhere the inmate spent his life from youth to incarceration is not\nstated. The Levis and Ernst chapter referenced below (1) reports\nthat in 2002, WHO reported over 600 000 new cases of leprosy, also\ncalled Hansen's disease (which as Dr. Frankel states is the preferred\nterminology), with the largest number of cases originating in South\nAsia (India, Bangladesh, Nepal and Myanmar). Africa and the Americas\neach reported about 40 000 new cases, with most of the Americas cases\nfrom Brazil.\n\nLevis and Ernst also note that _Mycobacterium leprae_ was the 1st\nbacterium to be associated with a disease. The Norwegian Armauer\nHansen discovered the organism in 1874 just prior to Koch's discovery\nof the the tubercle bacillus. The inability to cultivate _M. leprae_\non artificial media (which persists today) allowed Koch to be\ncredited with the discovery opening the germ theory of disease.\n\nIn the USA, _M. leprae_ is known to have been transmitted in several\nstates: Louisiana, Texas, Hawaii, and possibly California. 2 cases\nhave been reported, however, in residents of the eastern USA who had\nno obvious history of relevant travel or exposure (2).\n\n1. Levis WR, Ernst JD: Mycobacterium leprae (Leprosy, Hansen's\ndisease). In, Mandell, Douglas, and Bennett's Principles and Practice\nof Infectious Diseases, Mandell GL, Bennett JE, Dolin R (eds). 6th\ned., Elsevier Churchill Livingstone, Philadelhia, PA, 2005, pp\n2886-96.\n\n2. Levis WR, Vides EA, Cabrera A: Leprosy in the eastern United\nStates. JAMA 200; 283:1004-5.\n- Mod.LL]\nSee Also\nLeprosy - USA 20050525.1448\nLeprosy - Nepal (Tanahu) (02) 20050516.1339\nLeprosy - Nepal (Tanahu) 20050515.1336\n2003\n----\nLeprosy - Malawi 20030831.2193\n1998\n----\nLeprosy - Papua New Guinea (Bougainville) (05) 19980209.0257\nLeprosy - Papua New Guinea (Bougainville) 19980115.0108\n................ll/pg/jw\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Roberts, a researcher from the Institute of Food and\nAgricultural Sciences, University of Florida, has no idea how it arrived.\nShe and her partners were trying to conduct an unrelated experiment when\ntheir own crop was infected. Many Immokalee growers may be forced out of\nbusiness.\nAccording to Roberts, some affected fields are losing 100 per cent of their\nproduction. With few clues to go on, scientists say it's very likely this\nsituation will get worse.\nConsumers can identify an infected melon by examining the rind. Healthy\nwatermelons have a nice, white rind on the inside, but infected ones are\nyellow.\n[byline: Jessica Ritter]\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[There are a number of vine-decline diseases of cucurbits. The pathogen causes wheat leaves to yellow\nand die, but it's not caused by wheat streak mosaic, head death or\nfreezing.\n\"We're pretty sure it's a virus,\" said wheat breeder Joe Martin, who\nworks at the Kansas State University research station at Hays. \"It\nshowed up early and, at first glance, we thought it was wheat streak\nmosaic. But it's not. It kills the oldest leaves of the plant and\nfinally kills the head.\" Researchers don't know what the virus is,\nwhere it came from, or how it spreads.\nMartin said he's seen evidence of the virus in almost every field\nhe's checked in western Kansas, but it hasn't taken over the crop. He\nencouraged farmers to be on the lookout when checking their fields.\nDallas Seifers, a plant pathology professor at Fort Hays State\nUniversity, is trying to determine how the pathogen works, and what\nit might be.\n\"It's possible that this is something that has been identified\nsomewhere else in the world, even something that has shown up in a\ndifferent crop, corn or rice or something,\" Seifers said.\nSeifers, with the help of some virologists in Winnipeg, Canada, is\ntrying to identify the protein that causes the virus' symptoms. That\nwork is complicated by the fact that most affected plants found in\nthe field are already dead. Seifers is trying to grow his own supply\nof infected plants to study, but the effort hasn't been as successful\nas he hoped.\nOnce researchers identify the protein's genetic sequence, they can\ncompare it to known pathogens to find a match, or one that is in the\nsame family.\nSeifers said it's possible that the virus is showing up now because\nof recent unusual weather patterns, and that it wouldn't show up in a\nnormal year.\n\"We're just happy that it is not in large enough numbers to have an\neconomic impact this year, and we hope it will be spotty if it shows\nup next year,\" he said.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[A 28-kD protein has been specifically associated with infected\nplants. More research is needed to determine the mode of spread of\nthe pathogen. Also needed is more information about the protein and\nits relationship to disease expression. - Mod.DH]\n.......................................mpp/dh/pg/lm\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. For assistance from a\nhuman being send mail to: owner-majordomo@promedmail.org.\n############################################################\n############################################################", "reports": [{"diseases": ["unknown"], "syndromes": [], "event_date": ["2003-04-14 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Santarem", "country": "Portugal"}, {"location": "Brazil", "country": "Brazil"}, {"location": "FATAL", "country": "Chile"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2202751", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Coral reef kills, unknown etiology - Australia", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2202751", "date": "2002-10-23 23:50:00", "main_text": "CORAL REEF KILLS, UNKNOWN ETIOLOGY - AUSTRALIA\n**********************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail, a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\nDate: 22 Oct 2002\nFrom: A-Lan Banks <A-Lan.Banks@derwent.co.uk>\nSource: Environmental News Service [edited]\n<http://ens-news.com/ens/oct2002/2002-10-18-02.asp>\n\nMystery Disease Kills Coral on Great Barrier Reef\n------------------\nScientists at the Australian Institute of Marine Science (AIMS) have\nconfirmed the existence of coral disease on the world's longest reef,\nthe Great Barrier Reef, which stretches along Australia's east coast.\nResearchers at the government institute do not have to travel far to\nsee the giant reef (they just glance out their front door) but they\nhave searched the world for a clue to the cause of the disease they\nhave observed. AIMS scientists working in the long-term monitoring\nprogram say the disease they have documented is in a broad category\nknown as White Syndrome. Soon they hope to learn exactly what White\nSyndrome is.\nCathie Page, a deep ocean ecologist on the AIMS long term monitoring\nteam, says, \"It's more common on table corals. It starts at the base\nand works its way up. The disease breaks the coral tissue down,\neventually killing it.\"\n\"It doesn't fit the description of diseases found anywhere else in\nthe world, so it might be specific to the Pacific,\" she says. In the\n3 years Page has been on the monitoring team, she has logged about\n450 dives spanning 48 reefs. She has seen White Syndrome kill at\nvarying rates.\n\"It could kill a colony of 2 metres (6.5 feet) in diameter in 2 weeks\nbut in some other cases, it takes months to kill a large colony,\" she\nsaid.\nThe first record of coral diseases came from reefs off Belize and\nFlorida in 1973. In 1993 coral diseases were noticed on the Great\nBarrier Reef. When the diseases worsened in the late 1990s, the\nlong-term monitoring team started documenting their activity. In\n1999 only 7 reefs were infected with White Syndrome; in 2002 33\nreefs were affected out of the 48 studied by the AIMS long-term\nmonitoring team.\nThe highest number of infected colonies within one reef was 101 in a\n1500-square-meter area. That was on Carter Reef, an outer shelf reef\nin the Cooktown/Lizard Island sector. The syndrome killed those\ncolonies infected and caused a decline in hard coral cover on this\nreef.\nAIMS scientists together with researchers from James Cook University\nwho are collaborating on the project have recorded the disease in\nnorthern waters during the winter months. Outer-shelf reefs near\nLizard Island off Cooktown in the northern Great Barrier Reef and the\nCapricorn Bunker reefs in the southern Great Barrier Reef are the\nworst affected areas.\nWhite Syndrome outbreaks are happening in pristine areas of the Great\nBarrier Reef, the AIMS teams says, on outer reefs untouched by\ncoastal development and tourism. This means the reef diseases are not\nlinked to pollution, as are other coral diseases around the world.\nCoral bleaching is also affecting the great reef, and scientists fear\nWhite Syndrome could be spreading more quickly in corals weakened by\nbleaching.\nCoral bleaching is the name given to an event in which coral expel\ntheir symbiotic algae due to extreme stress, such as unusually hot\nwater, according to AIMS bleaching expert Dr. Terry Done.\nThe bleached corals die if the stress is extreme or prolonged. With\nrising water temperatures over the tropical summers, coral bleaching\nevents are more widespread and happening more often, leaving little\ntime for coral to recover.\n\"Bleached coral is not healthy and potentially more susceptible to\ndiseases,\" said Page. \"We don't know what's causing this disease.\nIt's microscopic; it could be a range of things.\"\nAIMS has sent samples of corals affected by White Syndrome away for\ntesting. When the results come back, AIMS will search for solutions\nthat might save the corals of the Great Barrier Reef.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[The coral reef bleaching is a serious problem for the ocean life\nthat live in and around the reef, but the devastation of the reef has\nconsequences for the land mass that the reef protects as well. There\nare many theories, including the rise in ocean water temperatures as\nreflected by our warming climate, a potential effect of El Nino, and\neven contamination due to human waste. It may be a combination of\nthese that has allowed the problem to escalate, but whatever the\ncause, it is affecting the reefs on almost a worldwide basis. -\nMod.TG]\nSee Also\nCoral reef kills, human waste suspected - Caribbean 20020627.4622\nBlack water, unknown cause - USA (Florida): RFI 20020321.3787\n2000\n----\nCoral reef kills - Belize 20000510.0713\n1999\n----\nCoral reef kills & potential human disease (02) 19990204.0161\nCoral reef kills & potential human disease 19990124.0111\n1998\n----\nCoral reef bleaching & El Nino - Indian Ocean (02) 19981113.2187\nRed tide advisory - Brunei 19980820.1668\nCoral reef bleaching, El Nino effects - Indian Ocean 19980705.1246\nCoral reef kills, etiology determined - USA 19980415.0693\n.............................tg/pg/lm\n*##########################################################*\n* *\n* Please support the 2002 ProMED-mail Internet-a-thon! *\n* http://www.isid.org/netathon2002.shtml *\n* *\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. A sixth person was treated\nand released. The six went into respiratory arrest after eating dinner in a\nYonkers apartment. Others in the apartment who did not eat the meal did not\nbecome sick, the police said.\nBecause an ambulance worker developed a rash after lifting one of the\nvictims, the police initially thought the incident could have been a far\nmore serious outbreak or attack. The rash is now believed to have been\neither a coincidence or a result of contact with a victim's bodily fluids.\nUntil medical officials can pinpoint the cause of the illness, the\nauthorities are treating the case as a possible crime. \"We are looking at\nsubstances in the food,\" said the Yonkers police commissioner, Charles A.\nCola. \"There are preservatives that can cause these symptoms. But at this\nlate date, we can say it wasn't cyanide.\"\nThe police responded to a call at 6:17 Thursday night for what appeared to\nbe an incident of food poisoning in a modest four-story row house, not far\nfrom the city's downtown. But because of the severity of the symptoms and\nthe skin reaction of the medical technician, city officials went into full\ncrisis mode.\nThe Federal Bureau of Investigation, the Westchester County district\nattorney's office and a team of hazardous materials experts were all called\nin. The neighborhood surrounding the apartment building was closed off, as\nwas St. Joseph's emergency room. And the American Red Cross started to set\nup an emergency shelter at a nearby church.\n\"We're dealing with so many unknowns in our society, as we're all painfully\naware of,\" Mayor John D. Spencer said at a news conference. \"As a\ngovernment, as a city, we have to be prepared to be overly cautious when\ndealing with issues such as chemicals or strange occurrences, which this\nwas yesterday.\"\nThe six victims \u0097 four men and two women of Egyptian heritage who range in\nage from 23 to 60 \u0097 apparently used some ingredients that were brought to\nthe United States from Egypt, Commissioner Cola said. \"Right now I'm\nleaning toward this being a non-crime, based on medical advice that it\ncould have happened naturally,\" he said.\nThere was no move to recall any particular food item because a number of\ningredients were used in the dinner. \"We don't know what it is, so how do I\nsay, `It's the A.& P.'?\" Commissioner Cola said.\nDr. Nicholas E. DeRobertis, director of emergency services at St. Joseph's\nMedical Center, said the list of substances that could provoke such a\nreaction was \"pretty extensive,\" including certain antibiotics and food\npreservatives. The patients suffered from a condition he called\nmethemoglobinemia, which changes the red blood cells so that they cannot\ncarry oxygen to the body's tissues.\n\"When that happens, people come in with severe respiratory distress, a\nbluish color and altered mental state,\" Dr. DeRobertis said at the news\nconference.\nMr. Cola said the patients were immediately given an antidote for cyanide\npoisoning as a precaution.\nFoods from the meal were being tested at a federal Food and Drug\nAdministration laboratory in Queens, said Heather McGill, a spokeswoman for\nthe Westchester County Health Department. It was not known when the test\nresults would be made available, she said.\n[Byline: Lisa W. Foderaro]\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[2]\nDate: Date: Fri, 17 May 2002 16:14:11 -0700\nFrom: \"Carol H. Knott\" <ctn43074@centurytel.net>\nSource: Newsday [edited]\nhttp://www.newsday.com/news/local/wire/ny-bc-ny--mysteriousillness0517may17.story?coll=ny%2Dap%2Dregional%2Dwire\n\nYONKERS, N.Y. -- Doctors blamed tainted food Friday for the sudden\nillnesses that set off a chemical-contamination scare in downtown\nYonkers. The police commissioner said he doubted there had been any foul\nplay. Two of the victims remained in critical condition a day after the\noutbreak of a rare blood-cell disease. Three were in stable condition and\none was released from the hospital.\n\"I'm leaning toward non-crime based on medical advice,\" said Commissioner\nCharles Cola. \"Nobody has told us this is something that somebody put in\nthe food.\" He added, however, that police were still treating the victims'\napartment as a crime scene and that \"It would be presumptuous of me to rule\nout foul play.\"\nMayor John Spencer said Yonkers was \"back to normalcy\" after spinning into\nemergency mode Thursday night. The six victims had been taken to St.\nJoseph's Medical Center with severe breathing problems, a bluish pallor and\nan altered mental state. Some emergency workers reported picking up a rash\nas they treated the patients, adding to the concern.\nThe hospital was closed briefly and police, firefighters,\nhazardous-materials workers and even anti-terrorist agents streamed into\nthe area. The mayor mentioned the possibility of cyanide poisoning. A\ndecontamination shower was set up outside the victims' home. Air tests in\nthe building and at the hospital found no airborne contamination, however.\nWhen police learned that people in the apartment who had not eaten did not\nget sick, the investigation focused on the food. Cola said the victims,\nwho were identified only as being adults and of Egyptian heritage, were at\na dinner party featuring a dish with many ingredients, including a\nprocessed food that they had brought in from Egypt.\n\"They became concerned about the taste of the food and quickly became ill,\"\nhe said. He said the investigation was concentrating \"on the apartment and\nthe food itself and the way it was prepared,\" rather than looking into\nwhether the victims had enemies who might have tampered with the food.\nDr. Nicholas DeRobertis, emergency director at St. Joseph's, said something\nthe victims ingested had changed their red blood cells into abnormal cells\nthat blocked the transfer of oxygen to tissue, a condition called\nmethemoglobinemia. It is unlike ordinary food poisoning, he said, in that\nit is a respiratory rather than gastrointestinal illness. He said he had\nseen it only once before, 15 or 20 years ago.\nThe doctor said the condition has more than 100 known causes, including\nunsafe levels of nitroglycerin; the antibiotics known as sulfonamides; or\nnaphthalene, which is used in mothballs. It is also used in some food\npreservatives, he said, but in that form would have to be ingested in \"very\nlarge quantities\" to inflict the illness. He said tests that might show if\nthe food was tampered with could take weeks. The mayor said it was\npremature to try recalls or other warnings about tainted food because\n\"there could be 100 different ways\" that the toxin got into the food.\n[By Jim Fitzgerald]\n--\nCarol H. Knott\n<ctn43074@centurytel.net>\n\n[The earlier reports on this event suggested cyanide poisoning and then\nlater botulism. The present reports state that the clinical picture was\nconsistent with methemoglobinemia. Cyanide poisoning can present with a\nclinical picture similar to that seen with acquired methemoglobinemia. The\nearlier report on possible botulism is difficult to understand other than a\nclinical presentation of respiratory distress. A good review of\nmethemoglobinemia can be found at the Vanderbilt Medical College website:\n<http://www.mc.vanderbilt.edu/peds/pidl/hemeonc/index.htm>\nMethemoglobin is an abnormal hemoglobin that is not capable of carrying\noxygen or carbon dioxide. In adults and normal children the level of\nmethemoglobin is less than 1% of the total hemoglobin. Acquired\nmethemoglobinemia (presence of methemoglobin in the blood) results when the\nrate of formation of methemoglobin exceeds the rate of reduction after\nexposure to certain compounds. Nitrites and aniline derivatives are\nsubstances which most frequently produce methemoglobinemia. Because of its\nuse as a meat preservative and its similarity in appearance to table salt,\nsodium nitrite is frequently the cause of methemoglobinemia. Nitrites are\nalso present in many medicinal agents. Mosts reports of nitrate- induced\nmethemoglobinemia, however, are due to infants fed well water contaminated\nwith high levels of nitrates. Nitrate salts are converted by enteric\nbacteria to nitrites which are then rapidly absorbed from the\ngastrointestinal tract. Silver nitrate used on burns is also a common\nsource as bacteria infecting burns may similarly cause nitrite production.\nAniline and its derivitives are found in many household products such as\ninks, shoe polishes, paints and varnishes, and in some red and orange wax\ncrayons. Other drugs commonly associated with methemoglobinemia include\nphenacetin, sulfonamides, and pyridium. Benzocaine, a common ingredient of\ntopical anesthetics has also been known to cause marked cyanosis from\nmethemoglobinemia.\nSymptoms of methemoglobinemia include headaches, fatigue, coma, and death.\nHeadache, fatigue, tachycardia (fast heartrate), weakness and dizziness do\nnot appear until levels reach 30- 40%. At 50- 60% oxygenation of tissues\nbecomes truly inadequate resulting in dyspnea (shortness of breath),\nacidosis, arrhythmias (irregular heart rhythms), coma, and convulsions.\nDeath occurs with concentrations of about 70% and greater. Treatment\ninvolves general supportive care and removal of any offending toxins. The\ndrug of choice for treating severe methemoglobinemia is methylene\nblue. Severe cases may require exchange transfusion or the use of\nascorbic acid.\nMethemoglobinemia has been associated with ingestion of food products\ncontaining toxic levels of nitrites/nitrates or other substances. An\noutbreak in India (ref.1) was associated with ingestion of rice\ncontaminated with metanil yellow used for coloring the rice. Other\noutbreaks have been associated with the use of excessive amounts of sodium\nnitrate (saltpetre) in the preparation of sausages and other meat products\n(refs. 2,3).\nMore information on the etiology of the methemoglobinemia in this outbreak\nwould be appreciated.\n1: Sachdeva SM, Mani KV, Adaval SK, Jalpota YP, Rasela KC, Chadha\nDS. Acquired toxic methaemoglobinaemia. J Assoc Physicians India. 1992\nApr;40(4):239-40.\n2: Kennedy N, Smith CP, McWhinney P. Faulty sausage production causing\nmethaemoglobinaemia. Arch Dis Child. 1997 Apr;76(4):367-8.\n3: Bacon R. Nitrate preserved sausage meat causes an unusual food\npoisoning incident. Commun Dis Rep CDR Rev. 1997 Mar 7;7(3):R45-7.\n4: Chan TY. Food-borne nitrates and nitrites as a cause of\nmethemoglobinemia. Southeast Asian J Trop Med Public Health. 1996\nMar;27(1):189-92. - Mod.MPP]\nSee Also\nUnknown illness, toxic ingestion suspected - USA (NY): RFI 20020517.4241\n....................................................mpp\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Illness Mystery\n--------------------------------------------------------------------------------\n\nYONKERS, N.Y. (AP) -- Six people became suddenly and violently ill in an\napartment, sparking a chemical-contamination scare that led to the\nevacuation of the building and the deployment of hazardous materials\nworkers and anti-terrorist agents.\n\nOfficials said later that the illness was probably caused by something in\nthe victims' food, but the outbreak was still being treated as a possible\ncrime.\n\n``We're leaning toward some kind of food poisoning because of the violent\nway they got sick,'' Police Commissioner Charles Cola said Friday. He\nmentioned botulism as one disease that might match the symptoms.\n\nTwo people were in critical condition and on life support, Mayor John\nSpencer said. The others were listed as stable, said Dr. Nicholas\nDeRobertis of St. Joseph's Medical Center.\n\n``It's definitely something they ingested, that they ate or drank, but we\ndon't know what,'' he said. But tests showed no\nairborne contamination or contagion, Spencer said. He urged residents to be\ncalm, saying, ``It looks like everything is going to be all right.''\n\nThe New York City anti-terrorism task force was called in Thursday night\n``due to the sensitivity of it and the times we live in,'' the mayor said.\n\nSpencer initially said the illness seemed to be caused by ``some kind of\npoison,'' and cyanide was considered a possibility. Cola said the victims'\nconditions deteriorated quickly.\n\nThe victims, whose names were not released, had eaten together in the\napartment less than a mile north of the New York city line. Other people in\nthe apartment who had not eaten did not become ill, the mayor said.\n\nResidents of the 18-unit building, including several children, were taken\nto an auditorium and tended by Red Cross workers until police allowed them\nto return home before dawn.\n[By: Jim Fitzgerald]\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[Initial reports on this outbreak were pointing a finger at cyanide\npoisoning as the etiology, justifying the speculation based on the rapidity\nof decline of the cases involved. They've dubbed it\n\"black water\", and have called local, state, and national government\nagencies to investigate.\nScientists who have heard of the phenomenon say they need answers too.\"It's\nkilled a lot of the bottom because recently a lot of little bottom plants\nare coming to the surface dead and rotten out in the Gulf,\" said Tim\nDaniels, 58, a Marathon Key fish-spotting pilot who has been flying over\nthe Gulf for more than 20 years. Experienced fishermen say they've often\nseen red tide but they've never seen anything like this \"it doesn't have a\nfoul smell, it isn't red tide and it isn't oil. They describe it as viscous\nand slimy water with what looks like spider webs in it.\"\nFirst sighted in January, the mass of black-colored water reached from 20\nmiles north of Marathon Key halfway to Naples. It stretched west almost 20\nmiles into the Gulf of Mexico.\nThough somewhat smaller now than descriptions from January, the mass of\nwater is still quite large and is moving into the Florida Keys National\nMarine Sanctuary, a 2800 square-mile sanctuary adjacent to the Keys, which\nis the largest coral reef in the United States. It includes the productive\nwaters of Florida Bay, the Gulf of Mexico, and the Atlantic Ocean. Part of\nthe ecosystem is an extensive nursery, feeding, and breeding ground,\nsupporting a variety of marine species and a multimillion dollar fishing\nindustry bringing in almost 20 million pounds of seafood each year.\nBilly Causey, superintendent of the sanctuary, told the Naples Daily News\nrecently that there is real concern in the scientific community about the\noverall health of the Gulf. Causey said contributing to the problems\nafflicting the shallow body is global warming, extended periods when the\nGulf waters aren't cooling in the winter, and the growing impact of human\nactivity along coastlines. Those problems, beginning in the early 1980s,\ninclude more frequent and longer lasting coral bleaching events that by\n1990 were affecting stouter coral reefs closer to shore and more adapted to\nwide temperature swings.\n\"There are places that are still beautiful but the shallow reefs would make\nyou cry,\" said Causey, a Keys diver since the 1950s. Scientists with Mote\nMarine Laboratory based in Sarasota said they are aware of the black water\nphenomenon but have not yet been able to test water samples. Mote is\nwilling to send out testing kits to fishermen who might encounter the black\nwater zone but, in the absence of a kit, fishermen could put a sample in a\nclean bottle and keep it in a cool, dark place until they could get it to a\nlab.\nDaniels said he first noticed the black water when he went out in\nmid-January, ahead of kingfish season, to see what fishermen had in store\nfor 2002. When he was flying over water that was 50 feet deep and north of\nthe Keys, Daniels began to notice a change in the water color. When pilots\nfrom the air see boats move through a red tide zone, they often cut the\nreddish or brownish water to reveal green below. That doesn't occur in the\nblack water. \"This (dark) stuff goes all the way to the bottom,\" Richardson\nsaid. Boats that have 4 to 5 feet of hull below the surface cut through 35\nto 40 feet of water and leave nothing but the same black water in their\nwakes. It's the same at depths of 15 feet, he said.\nAlong with the newly discovered black water and coral bleaching, there have\nbeen other problems with the Gulf having been documented for years. They\ninclude a New Jersey-sized dead zone coming off the Mississippi River\noutlet to the Gulf that consumes a larger area each summer. There are\nincidences of a contamination known as fibro papilloma [any information\nabout this condition? - Mod.SH] in green turtles that live in Florida Bay.\nThere are other problems in shrimp and an entire marine ecosystem appears\nto be sick.\n\u00ad\u00ad\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[The black water is a mystery requiring more water testing but apparently\ndefying life. There were 16 people doing volunteer\nwork at the monument, half of them digging in an Indian sweat cave off the\nmonument's main road and the other half working on the quarter-mile trail\nleading to the cave, called the \"Swelter Shelter.\"\nThe 8 volunteers, including one from inside the cave and 7 from the trail,\nwere taken to Ashley Valley Medical Center in Vernal. All but 2 were\nreleased Tuesday, according to Joseph B. Shaffer, director of the\nTri-County Health Department, which is investigating the mysterious\nillness. The 2 still in the hospital are being held for observation at\nleast through Wednesday.\nHealth officials ruled out bubonic plague, hantavirus [infection, and\ndeer-fly bites [which are known to transmit tularemia as possible causes.\nThe volunteers have been treated for fungal and bacterial infection,\nShaffer said. The fungal infection comes from the soil, with no possible\nperson-to-person transmission.\nAll 16 of the volunteers who became ill high school graduates and college\nstudents were from outside the area. They came from Alaska, Florida,\nKansas, Vermont, Virginia, and one from Poland. Volunteers from Utah and\nColorado were not affected. \"My suspicion is we develop a lot of immunities\nto things that knock people out who are not living here,\" Shaffer said.\nThe investigation team includes representatives from the Centers for\nDisease Control and Prevention and local and state health officials. Collins\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[The preceding article does not specify the clinical syndromes other than\nto mention \"flu-like illness\" and suggest the involvement of a fungal\netiology. Histoplasmosis, coccidioidomycosis, and blastomycosis are\nillnesses that present with flu-like symptoms, and are infections with\ndimorphic fungi found in the soil in the USA, and could be responsible for\nthese outbreaks.\nHistoplasmosis is caused by _Histoplasma capsulatum_ . According to Gideon\n<http://www.gideononline.com>: histoplasmosis was first reported in the\nUnited States in 1926. The disease is endemic to the Ohio, Mississippi, and\nMissouri River valley areas, with highest rates in Kentucky, Illinois,\nIndiana, Missouri, Ohio, and Tennessee. Although the endemic area for\nblastomycosis overlaps that of histoplasmosis, blastomycosis is also found\nfurther north, and not as far to the west as histoplasmosis. In 1969, it\nwas estimated that 40 million Americans were infected [with\nhistoplasmosis, with 200 000 cases of acute pulmonary disease per year.\nSkin test-positivity rates approach 95% in areas of South Kentucky and\nMiddle Tennessee; 80% to 90% in Kansas City (adults over age 20, 1963).\nCoccidioidomycosis is caused by _Coccidioides immitis_. According to\nGideon: Coccidioidomycosis was first reported in the United States in 1893:\nThe disease is most common in California (the San Joaquin Valley), Arizona,\nTexas, New Mexico, southern Nevada, and Utah. The highest incidence occurs\nin late summer and early fall -- large outbreaks frequently follow dust\nstorms. An estimated 100 000 infections occur in the United States\nannually, and 1 in 200 infections progresses to disseminated disease.\nBlastomycosis is caused by _Blastomyces dermatitidis_. Most cases are\nreported from Arkansas, Kentucky, Mississippi, North Carolina, South\nCarolina, and Wisconsin. Infection was first reported in Colorado in 1998.\nAlthough the endemic area for blastomycosis overlaps that of\nhistoplasmosis, blastomycosis is also found further north, and not as far\nto the west as histoplasmosis.\nBy geographic distribution, coccidioidomycosis would be the highest on the\nlist as possible etiologies followed by blastomycosis and then\nhistoplasmosis. Additional information on this outbreak would be\nappreciated. - Mod.MPP\n[While we are putting forward our best guesses, the history of cave\nexploration is of interest. Cave-associated histoplasmosis is well known to\noccur among spelunkers exposed to bat excreta. - Man. Ed. Pollack <pollackmp@mindspring.com>\nSource: Reuters Online, 27 Oct 2000 [edited]\n\nStudy: Oregon River Water Causing Fish Deformities\n--------------------------------------------------\nPORTLAND, Oregon - Deformed fish found for years in a stretch of Oregon's\nWillamette River were damaged by something in the water, not by genetic\ndefects as had previously been believed, according to a new study\nreleased on Friday.\nScientists, who called their findings \"shocking,\" said they were\nworking to determine the exact cause of the problem, and investigating\nwhether there were health risks associated with eating the deformed fish\nfound in a 30-mile section of the river.\n\"It could be chemical, bacterial, parasites, high river temperature,\nlow oxygen levels, a variety of things,\" said Steve Ellis, a scientist\nwith EVS Environment Consultants, the Seattle-based firm conducting the\nstudy.\n\"We need to continue to study this and determine the cause and sources\nof the problem and then do something about it, which we will do,\" said\nDick Pedersen, manager of the watershed management section of Oregon's\nDepartment of Environmental Quality.\nThe study was conducted near a stretch of the Willamette between Newberg\nand Oregon City, scientists said. There, 3500 rainbow trout eggs from a\nCalifornia trout farm were left above a river bed for 2 weeks until eyes\ncould be seen. They were then taken to a lab, reared to the fry stage,\nand studied against trout raised only in a laboratory.\nOf 372 trout sampled, 34 percent exposed to Willamette River water for 2\nweeks had deformities compared with 9 percent of the fish raised in the\nlaboratory, the study found.\n\"The types of skeletal deformities we saw are the same type as with\nother fish\" having come out of the Willamette, EVS's Ellis said. In\naddition, prior studies have shown random samples of fish from the this\npart of the river have had deformity rates above 70 percent, scientists\nsaid.\nAbout 70 percent of Oregon's population lives within 20 miles of the\nWillamette River, making it an important symbol for Oregonians, Allen\nsaid, adding, \"This is a slap upside the head for Oregonians.\"\nThe river, which winds through the state's most populated areas, is also\nplanned as a source of drinking water for the town of Wilsonville in\n2002.\nEllis's firm is working on a study of the health risks of eating fish\ncaught on this stretch of the Willamette.\n\"We are still dumping a lot of chemicals in this part of the river,\"\nAllen said. \"A lot of people think this is stuff that happened 40 years\nago. Dumping [is] a current problem.\"\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n[If they knew who was dumping chemicals, then something could perhaps\nmore easily be done. Pollack <pollackmp@mindspring.com>\nSource: AP Online, 31 Aug 2000 12:38 PM EDT [edited]\n\nUkraine Region Declared Disaster\n---------------------------------\nKIEV, Ukraine (AP) -- President Leonid Kuchma on Thursday declared 4\nsouthern Ukrainian villages an ecological disaster zone, following an\noutbreak of a mysterious illness some have blamed on leaks of Soviet-era\nrocket fuel.\nAbout 400 residents of the villages near Mykolaiv, 320 miles south of the\ncapital Kiev, have been reported sick since 4 Jul 2000, but only a few new\ncases were registered this week, Health Minister Vitaliy Moskalenko said.\nHealth officials were checking nearly 6 000 residents of nearby villages\nfor signs of the illness, a chemical poisoning [it is not established on\nthe basis of this and the next report that the illness is due to chemical\nexposure -Man. DS] that causes skin rashes and affects the liver and\npancreas, he told a state investigative commission.\nHigh concentrations of nitrates have been found in 57 percent of drinking\nwater supplied through a pipe system, 98 percent of water from wells and\nevery tenth food product checked, Moskalenko said in comments cited by the\nInterfax news agency.\nOfficials said Ukraine would appeal to the World Health Organization and\nforeign governments to help it deal with the emergency. The government,\nmeanwhile, appeared split on what caused the illness, with some officials\nblaming rocket fuel and others denying the possibility. In the region is a\nbase holding solid-fuel SS-24 nuclear missiles, which are to be dismantled\nin 2001. It once housed other Soviet missiles powered by liquid fuel.\nHealth experts have said the area's soil and water contained substances\nthat are usually produced by decomposing rocket fuel, but Kuchma's\nspokesman Oleksandr Martynenko insisted on Wednesday that rocket fuel could\nnot be blamed. Instead, he suggested that the illness could have resulted\nfrom high amounts of nitrates in the soil. But the head of the government\ncommission, deputy premier Mykola Zhulynskyi, said missile silos in the\narea had remained open after liquid-fuel rockets were dismantled there in\nthe late 1970s. Fuel spills could have occurred during the missiles'\ndismantling, he said. Ukraine inherited 46 SS-24 and 130 SS-19 nuclear\nmissiles following the 1991 Soviet collapse. Kiev has since surrendered all\nits nuclear warheads to Russia and eliminated the last SS-19 missile in\n1999. Most of the SS-24s along with missile silos remain to be destroyed\nunder a disarmament plan running through 2001.\n[Byline: Sergei Shargorodsky]\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n***\n[2]\nDate: 31 Aug 2000 20:38:33 -0400\nFrom: Marjorie P. COLI INFECTIONS, UNKNOWN SOURCE - USA (OHIO) (02)\n****************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.healthnet.org/programs/promed.html>\nSee Also\nE. coli infections, unknown source - USA (Ohio) 990804215402\nDate: Thu, 05 Aug 1999 10:48:40 -0400\nFrom: Marjorie P. Pollack <pollackmp@mindspring.com>\nSource: The Cincinnati Enquirer, 5 Aug 1999\n<http://enquirer.com/editions/1999/08/05/loc_foodborne_illness.html>\n\nA cluster of 20 recent cases of _E. coli_ sickness in Hamilton, Butler and\nClermont counties has triggered an investigation by state and federal\nhealth officials.\nAll 20 victims were infected with _E. coli_ O157:H7, a powerful, sometimes\ndeadly food-borne bacterium that strikes about 65,000 people a year\nnationwide. _E. coli_ from contaminated beef killed four people and\nsickened hundreds of others after they ate at Jack-in-the-Box restaurants\nin Washington state in 1993.\nTRISTATE CASES\nState and federal health officials are searching for what caused a\n\"cluster\" of 20 confirmed cases of _E. coli_ sickness in Greater Cincinnati\nin June and July. Key test results are expected by week's end.\n- Hamilton County: 8 confirmed cases including 2 in Loveland, 2 in Indian\nHill, 2 in Anderson township, one each in Blue Ash and Symmes Township.\n- Butler county: 6 confirmed cases, 3 probable cases; includes 4 in\nFairfield, 2 in Liberty Township, one each in Union and Madison townships.\n- Clermont County: 6 confirmed cases, mostly in Union and Miami Townships\nnear the Hamilton County line.\nThe Ohio Department of Health and county departments of health [have\nindicated that:\nno deaths have occurred in the local cluster, but several victims were sick\nenough to require hospital care.\nAlthough the investigation is in its early stages, officials do not think\nall 20 people got sick from a single source of contamination, such as a\ngroup picnic or restaurant.\n\"It is really very unlikely that it's a single source,\" said Janet\nRickabaugh, Clermont County health commissioner. \"[The investigation could\ntake several weeks. And we may never find out much more information on the\nsource.\"\nTracking the source of a foodborne illness can be complicated, health\nofficials say. People don't always accurately remember what they eat and\nfinding samples of contaminated food can be elusive.\nThe 20 cases include eight in Hamilton County, six in Clermont County and\nsix in Butler County. All were diagnosed in June or July.\nThat's an unusually high number, even though health officials say summer is\nthe peak season for _E. coli_ infections. Those three counties reported a\ntotal of 11 cases in 1998, [according to Ohio Department of Health\nspokeswoman Jane Beathard.\nStatewide, 87 cases of _E. coli_ illnesses were reported this year through\nJuly 28, compared with 54 cases in the same period a year ago.\n_E. coli_ O157:H7 is one of hundreds of strains of _E. coli_ bacteria, many\nof which pose no threat to human health. The dangerous strain is commonly\npassed by eating undercooked beef. However, _E. coli_ outbreaks also have\nbeen linked to unpasteurized milk and apple juice, and raw alfalfa sprouts.\nThe [infection can be spread by failing to wash hands after using the\nrestroom or changing diapers, or by drinking or swimming in contaminated\nwater.\nTo find the source or sources of the local cluster, county health\ndepartments have been interviewing victims about what they ate and where.\nBacterial samples collected from the victims have been sent to the state\nhealth department for closer analysis.\nThree experts from the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention, brought\nin at the request of the state health department, arrived Wednesday. They\nwill be working from the Clermont County Health Department.\nBased on interviews with the people who became sick, food samples have been\ncollected from at least two restaurants, but no test results were available\nWednesday.\nThe state health department expects to have results by week's end from a\ntype of bacteria DNA testing called pulsed field gel electrophoresis.\nEven _E. coli_ O157:H7 can vary in subtle ways. The DNA test can tell much\nlike a fingerprint whether any of the 20 people caught the same variation\nof the strain.\nThe lack of common circumstances among the cases makes it likely that more\nthan one source of contamination was involved.\nIn Clermont County, none of the six victims were from the same family. In\nButler County, ages of victims ranged from 4 to 82. In Hamilton County,\nvictims lived in five different cities or townships, with ages ranging from\n4 to 63.\n\"We've been trying to find a common food source, but so far we haven't\nfound one,\" said Patricia Burg, director of the Butler County Health\nDepartment.\nNo new cases have been reported for several days, but it is too soon to\ntell whether the cluster is likely to grow beyond the 20 cases, Ms.\nBeathard said. The potential risk to the public depends on the cause or\ncauses of the illness.\nFranklin County has reported four recent _E. coli_ cases near Columbus but\nstate officials do not believe those cases are linked to the Greater\nCincinnati cases. Officials also are investigating three cases reported in\nKentucky and one in Indiana to see if they might be linked to the local\ncluster.\n[Byline: Tim Bonfield\n--\nProMED-mail\ne-mail: promed@usa.healthnet.org\n[This article suggests this outbreak may be bigger than just the southern\nportion of Ohio. This compares\nwith nine cases throughout 1998 and nine in 1997. Some of the infections\nhave been serious: at least seven patients have been admitted to hospital\nand in four cases the organism has been isolated from blood. Most of the\ncases identified so far are from London and one case lives in Luton, which\nis some 30 miles north of London. Another case, who lives in Leicester (a\ncity in the Midlands), may have acquired the infection in London. Twelve of\nthe cases have Asian names, and some are known to be vegetarian. None had\nrecently travelled abroad. Six of the patients are adults, three are\nteenagers, and four are children aged between 5 months and 4 years.\nThe infecting strain is susceptible to antimicrobial agents, including\nampicillin, chloramphenicol, trimethoprim, and ciprofloxacin. _S. java_ is\na group B salmonella and has the same antigenic structure as S. paratyphi B\n(O = 4, 12: H = b : 1.2). _S. java_ and _S. paratyphi_ B are differentiated\nby the dextro-tartrate test, in which _S. java_ produces a positive acid\nreaction, whereas _S. paratyphi_ B is negative. The fact that several\npatients developed bacteraemia suggests that the organisms are behaving\nmore like _S. paratyphi_ B, which routinely causes enteric fever, rather\nthan _S. java_, which is more commonly associated with gastroenteritis.\nThe PHLS Communicable Disease Surveillance Centre (CDSC) is investigating\nthe contact details of the individual cases and asking them about food\nexposures. An enquiry is being made to Enter-net (the international\nsurveillance network for enteric pathogens), which will ask whether cases\nare also occurring in other countries and whether any foodstuffs have been\nidentified recently that may help in the investigation of this outbreak.\nReported by Sergio Brusin (sbrusin@phls.nhs.uk), PHLS Communicable Disease\nSurveillance Centre, Georgia Duckworth (g.duckworth@cdsc.nthames.nhs.uk),\nPHLS Communicable Disease Surveillance Centre Thames, Linda Ward\n(lward@phls.co.uk) PHLS Laboratory of Enteric Pathogens, London, England,\nand Ian Fisher (ifisher@phls.co.uk) Enter-net scientific coordinator.\n--\nProMED-mail\ne-mail: promed@usa.healthnet.org\n......................................es\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. The bacteria\nthat sickened people in the town of Alpine, home to 470 people, probably\ncame from cattle, elk or deer waste that found its way into the town's\nspring-fed water system.\nIt is only the second outbreak in the nation that has been linked to\nmunicipal water.\nA 1990 outbreak in rural Missouri killed four people and hospitalized 32\nothers; 21 people were confirmed to have been infected in that outbreak.\nHealth officials said the strain responsible for the Wyoming outbreak,\nwhich arose in late June, can lead to kidney failure, anemia, clotting\nproblems and in some cases, coma and death.\n--\nProMED-mail\ne-mail: promed@usa.healthnet.org\n......................................es\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. LaBudde <ral@lcfltd.com>\n\nSOURCE OF GUATEMALAN BERRY CONTAMINATION UNKNOWN-U.S.\nWASHINGTON -- Experts with the U.S. Centres for Disease Control and\nPrevention (CDC) were cited as telling a U.S. Senate panel investigating\nfood safety at a hearing on Thursday that scientists have yet to trace the\ncause of 2,500 cases of food poisoning from Guatemala raspberries, but\nwater used by local growers remains the most likely cause.\nThe story says that after disease outbreaks in 1996 and 1997, exports of\nthe raspberries were voluntarily halted by Guatemalan farmers. The Food and\nDrug Administration said it would ban 1998 exports until farms adopted a\nseries of precautions, such as filtering water used for irrigation and\npesticide spraying.\nThe story adds that Canada, which has continued to import berries from\nGuatemala, last month reported more than 200 additional illnesses that may\nbe connected to the raspberries (305 last I heard; doubt they will have\nsimilar public hearings in Canada -- dp)\nDr. Stephen Ostroff, an investigator with the CDC was qutoed as saying,\n\"The preliminary data suggests that these are once again linked to\nGuatemalan raspberries.\"\nCDC investigators are, the story says, working with the Canadian government\nto try to crack the latest cases there. But no laboratory methods yet exist\nto detect cyclospora on berries or in water, according to the CDC.\nSenator Susan Collins, a Maine Republican who heads a Senate subcommittee\nthat has been investigating the safety of imported food, was cited as\nsaying that most of the raspberry-linked illnesses occurred during\nGuatemala's spring rainy season. Such patients have been visiting the out-door\npatient departments (OPDs) and casualty departments of government hospitals\nin the city almost daily. However, most of the cases are being reported at\nprivate clinics of general physicians every day.\nAttributing the increase in the cases of gastroenteritis to the prevailing\nunhygienic conditions, a polluted environment and impure water, doctors\nsaid such patients complained of pain in the abdomen, vomited and suffered\nfrom diarrhoea. They advised people that as soon as they come across a\nperson having the above-mentioned symptoms, they must take him or her to a\nnearby hospital or clinic because excessive vomiting and diarrhoea lead to\ndehydration.\nConfirming that the cases of gastroenteritis which had shot up during May\nhave not yet subsided, doctors said as the cases of gastroenteritis would\ncontinue to increase till August, people must drink a sufficient quantity\nof boiled water, besides avoiding stale food and fruits. Rep. of Congo 971231005644\nDate: Sat, 17 Jan 1998 14:22:09\nFrom: Jerome Sclafer <jeromjet@easynet.fr>\n\nDr Bill Clemmer at Vanga Hospital wrote:\n>We [have had several replies about the cause of this unexplained\n>encephalopathy. The\nsuggestion is based on the combination of encephalopathy with aspirin\nintake reported for the cases and their association reported in the\nliterature.\nAspirin intake is, however, not a rare event; I guess that 90% of cases of\nencephalopathy of any cause in DR Congo would have had aspirin in their\npast few days history. Therefore, Reye's syndrome is not more likely than\nany other cause of encephalopathy. In addition, should aspirin not have\nbeen taken, Reye's syndrome would still remain one of the options.\nIt is interesting to see that physicians only suggest one not very likely\ndiagnosis, while a computer aid (like GIDEON in previous mail) reminds us\nthat the presentation of the patients is non-specific and suggests many\nmore alternative diagnoses: hemorrhagic fevers (Ebola, Marburg,\nCrimean-Congo), yellow fever, bacterial septicemia, malaria, anthrax and\nshigellosis.\n--\nJerome Sclafer\nMember of the editorial team\nLa revue Prescrire\nBP 459 - 75527 France Cedex 11\ntel: +33 1 4700 9445\nfax: +33 1 4807 8732\ne-mail: jeromjet@easynet.fr\n[Bill, do you have sera or other specimens, and can you get them out to CDC\nor Jo'burg for testing? - Mod.JW\n......................................jw/es\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Could these be cases of Reye syndrome?\n--\nPaul N. Goldwater FRACP, FRCPA,\nSenior Consultant Clinical Microbiologist,\nMicrobiology & Infectious Diseases Department,\nWomen's & Children's Hospital,\nNorth Adelaide,\nSouth Australia 5006.\nTEL: 61 8 8204 7432\nFAX: 61 8 8204 6051\ne-mail: pgoldwat@medicine.adelaide.edu.au\n\n[3]\nDate: Mon, 5 Jan 1998 21:24:05 -0000\nFrom: Ness & Nigel Hughes <D0070606@infotrade.co.uk>\n\nIs it possible that these two unfortunate cases are suffering from an\nextreme enteroviral infection, either Coxsackie ( A or B?), or enterovirus\ntype 70/71? They both had GI symptoms, both exhibited encephalopathy, CNS\nsymptoms such as hypertonia and hypereflexia, and coma.\nThe second case also exhibited pulmonary and cardiac problems, as well as a\nmild hepatosplenomegaly. They would fit a susceptible age group.\nThe only other similarities in these two cases are the use of aspirin\nprecipitating the onset of coma - Reye's Syndrome?\n--\nNigel Hughes RN\nClinical Nurse Specialist HIV & Virology\nMidKent Healthcare NHS Trust\nMaidstone\nKent UK\ne-mail: D0070606@infotrade.co.uk\n\n[4]\nDate: Tue, 6 Jan 1998 20:25:36 +0100\nFrom: Dennis Falzon <dennis.falzon@magnet.mt>\n\nHaving read the piece on the Vanga hospital paediatric cases I couldn't\nhelp but thinking of Reye's syndrome as a provisional diagnosis. Perhaps\n[the attending physicians] can [post mortem] or viscerotome (liver) the\nfirst case for fatty changes. Local doctors, healers and general public\nshould be warned about haphazard use of aspirin in febrile children. Will\nbe most interested to know of progress on these cases.\n--\nDr. D. REP. CONGO (03)\n*******************************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nSee Also\nEncephalopathy, etiology unknown - Dem. Rep. of Congo 971231005644\nEncephalopathy, etiology unknown - Dem. Rep. of Congo (02) 980101214555\nDate: Sun, 04 Jan 98 10:24:43 UTC\nFrom: William Clemmer <CLEMMER@MAF.Org>\n\n[Note: This message, when sent originally, was received truncated. The\nposting below is the complete version. - Mod.CHC\nThis report comes from Vanga Hospital in the Democratic Republic of Congo\n(former Zaire) where two cases of encephalopathy of undetermined etiology\npresented this week at this 400 bed mission hospital located in the\ninterior of the Republic of Congo 80 kilometers upriver from Kikwit.\nTwo school age children from different villages in the Vanga Health\nDistrict (5-7 kilometers apart) presented to the reference hospital with\nsymptoms consistent with a viral gastroenteritis. Both evolved rapidly\ninto a coma. The first child died within 48 hours, the second is at present\nstill comatose. The children attended different schools and secured water\nand food from different sources. No family members to date have contracted\nthis illness.\nThe cases are as follows:\nPatient 1 (hospitalized 12/18/97)\nThe first patient, an eight year old boy from the village Songo, 6 km from\nVanga fell ill on a Friday after school and died 2 days later on Sunday\nevening. The child in otherwise good health had been seen at Vanga\nHospital five days earlier for a sprained ankle sustained while playing\nsoccer. He was given aspirin and an ace bandage. On the first day of his\nillness he experienced colicky abdominal pain with persistent vomiting. He\nwas treated locally with chloramphenicol and an anti-spasmodic\n(papaverine). The following day he was referred to the Vanga Hospital\nwhere clinical exam by a physician suggested viral gastroenteritis in an\notherwise healthy child. He was afebrile, a malaria smear was negative and\nwhite blood count was 12,150mm3. The child was hospitalized for\nobservation. Chloramphenicol and an antispasmodic were continued.\nEarly the following morning the night nurse found the boy in a deep coma\nand signaled the physician on call. The child was febrile (38 C) with\ntachycardia (120) and tachypnea (48). He was unresponsive to all stimuli.\nPupils were dilated and responsive to light. Diffuse hypotonia and\nhyporeflexia were noted. Babinski test was negative. Lungs were clear and\nheart sounds normal. The liver edge was smooth and palpated 3cm below the\ncostal margin; spleen was not enlarged. No icterus was noted. Urine was\nnegative for bilirubin. A spinal tap revealed 2 white cells and 408 red\ncells, no trypanosomes were found on a centrifuged specimen. Malaria smear\nagain was negative. A blood sugar was 97. An anticholinesterase agent\n(neostigmine) was given without effect. The child was started empirically\non intravenous quinine and dextrose. He remained in a coma and died later\nthat day.\nPatient 2 (hospitalized 12/26/97)\nThe second child, a six-year-old girl from the village of Bilili, 11 km\nfrom Vanga developed a low grade fever, abdominal pain and vomiting 5 days\nprior to her presentation to this hospital. She was treated at a local\nclinic with Bactrim and aspirin and referred to the Vanga hospital when her\nsymptoms persisted. She walked into the outpatient clinic at Vanga on a\nSaturday afternoon and the following day, today is in a coma.\nThe child was evaluated by a physician upon entry and felt to have mild\ngastroenteritis but otherwise in good health. She was afebrile and had\nsubtle rales in her right lung base, though no cough or dyspnea were noted.\nHer malaria smear was negative, white count 6,800 mm3 and stool sample was\nnegative for leukocytes and parasites. She too was hospitalized under\nobservation and started on oral rehydration fluids and ampicillin.\nDuring the evening she had persistent vomiting and became combative.\nClinical exam by the same physician in the morning revealed her to be in a\ncoma, minimally responsive to noxious stimuli. She was afebrile with heart\nrate 90 and respiratory rate 30. Pupils were again dilated and responsive\nto light. She had diffuse hypertonia though a negative Babinski. No\nseizure activity was noted. Rales were now noted along both bases and an\nS3 gallop was appreciated. Liver and spleen were mildly enlarged. A spinal\ntap was negative with 1 white cell, 3 reds and no evidence of trypanosomes.\nRepeat malaria was negative. White count was now 15,900 with 85% polys\nand 15% lymphocytes (no eosinophils noted). Sedimentation rate was 103mm\n(elevated for our lab). The child was given furosemide with resolution of\nher rales and diminution of tachypnea. Quinine and dextrose were given\nI.V. Later in the day the child became diffusely hypotonic and\nunresponsive to all stimuli . As of this report, the child is still\ncomatose.\nPublic Health teams will travel tomorrow to these villages to interview\nfamily members and school mates. Similar cases (if any), treated in the\nlocal dispensary will be identified.\nBlood has been obtained from the second child and the serum separated and\nfrozen. These are available to an interested collaborator, although\narrangements need to be made with the State Department and then the U.S.\nEmbassy in Kinshasa for exportation. The samples can be sent frozen by\nMissionary Aviation Flights from Vanga to Kinshasa.\nWe will advise ProMED-mail of future cases. We welcome comments from\nProMED-mail subscribers and similar experiences by physicians as we try and\nidentify the etiology of this apparent rapid onset encephalopathy in two\nschool age children in our health district in the DRC, Central Africa.\n--\nDr Jacques Katele\nChief, Vanga Health Zone\nand\nDr William Clemmer\nStaff Physician, Vanga Hospital\nEvangelical Hospital\nDemocratic Republic of Congo\ne-mail: CLEMMER@MAF.Org\n......................................chc/es\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Rep. of Congo 971231005644]\nDate: Wed, 31 Dec 1997 18:03:52 -0800\nFrom: Steve Berger <mberger@post.tau.ac.il>\n\nI ran these cases through the diagnosis/simulation module of GIDEON.\nAssuming an infectious etiology, the constellation of coma, fever and\nvomiting is rather non-specific for a country like Democratic Republic of\nCongo. Additional data (rash? thrombocytopenia? conjunctivitis?\nlymphadenopathy? arthritis? animal contact?, etc) would be most helpful.\nAmong the diseases listed by GIDEON are hemorrhagic fevers (Ebola, Marburg,\nCrimean-Congo hemorrhagic fever, yellow fever - the latter last reported in\nthis country in 1972), bacterial septicemia, malaria, anthrax and Reye's\nsyndrome (interesting possibility, given that the patient received\naspirin). Shigellosis is also listed, and might conceivably have been\nworsened by adminstration of Papaverine. One might also consider an\nunusual reaction to chloramphenicol - the dosage and preparations used\nshould be reviewed.\n--\nSteve Berger\nTel Aviv medical Center\ne-mail: mberger@post.tau.ac.il\n......................................chc/es\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. One suggests a real world cause, one\nsuggests sorcery. As attempted sorcery is no less real than attempted\ndiagnosis, the reports are combined. - Mod.CHC]\n[1]\n[Digested] report by Dr Stephen Flew, Senior Medical Office, Ok Tedi Mining\nLtd, (written for Papua New Guinea Department of Health); Re: Outbreak of\nunknown disease /excess deaths, Sandaun Province\n[Investigation] indicated the [people] at Tapsie village were mostly\naffected by the usual skin afflictions common to the area such as grille\n(tinea imbicata), tropical ulcers, yaws, etc. as well as malaria,\nfilariasis, tropical splenomegaly and pneumonia. However there did not\nappear to be any excess of morbidity or mortality at this time, nor during\nthe drought. [The investigators] were told that the most affected areas were\nUrapmin and Ibikmen but that people had fled their usual dwellings due to\nfear of sorcery and were living in bush houses. They were strongly advised\nnot to go to these areas on foot by the people at Yapsie, because of the\ndangers of sorcery. The team were willing to go into Urapmin by chopper so\nwe flew them [there], accompanied by a local guide. The main village of\nUrapmin appeared deserted so we proceeded up the Aki river to an isolated\nhamlet and managed to land the chopper on a tiny beach in a nearby creek.\nThere seemed to be about 20-30 people living here, many covered in \"grille\"\nand with swollen bellies but seemed to be saying they were all OK, as far as\nI could make out above the noise of the chopper. The terrain here is very\nbroken and mountainous and I believe these people must be in a chronic state\nof poor health, with no ready access to any form of health care. There did\nnot appear to have been any deaths there but we left a team behind to\ninvestigate further.\nThe other team (from the Health Dept. in Vanimo) had gone to Ibikmin\nvillage. When we arrived they had already left; apparently there were no\nproblems there either.\nWe proceeded to another isolated hamlet on a heavily forested ridge, known\nas Finyaksolmin. The second team had only just arrived the night before and\nhad had no chance to check the local population yet. They were told there\nhad been no deaths here but 2 people had died at nearby Firayabamin, one\nolder woman of COAD [?] and a young man of malaria (?).\nBack at Yapsie [the health worker] who had been left in charge for the last\nfew months reported that health-wise things [were bad but] hadn't really got\nany worse during the drought, with a high prevalence of childhood\nmalnutrition (30-50%) and many patients with chronic anaemia and\nhepatosplenomegaly (secondary to filariasis and malaria). There is a high\nproportion of refugees now residing amongst the locals at Yapsie who have\n[come] from over the border with Irian Jaya (just 10 kms away). The\npresence of the refugees in an already marginally productive environment\nexacerbates the malnutrition problem, and malnutrition is accepted as the\nnorm for growing children. No doubt the maternal, infant and childhood\nmortality rates must be amongst the highest in PNG, even under normal\nclimatological conditions. Even in October Yapsie appeared fairly green and\nnot much affected by drought. Now the Sepik river is in flood and there is\nlittle evidence of drought except for the extensive bushfires in the high\ncountry between here and Tabubil.\nA review of the Health Centre monthly reports at Yapsie indicates there was\nnot much change in outpatient illness rates for the common conditions of\ncough, pneumonia, other respiratory disease, diarrhoea, malaria, anaemia,\nyaws and other skin diseases during October-November (total of 1200\nattendances each month). During the first 3 weeks of December there appears\nto have been an increase in malaria, 'flu, diarrhoea and skin diseases (the\nlatter esp. from Munbil, ?some ulcers requiring skin grafts). However there\nhas been no increase in deaths recorded at Yapsie and the [administrator]\nfrom Fiak was also surprised to hear of the 10 deaths which were reported\nfrom his area, none of which he was aware of.\nIt would appear that the lay reporting of 19 deaths and 24-30 people\ncritically ill or comatose in the Yapsie and Fiak subdistricts of Telefomin\nhas been grossly exaggerated. The reporting of these deaths may have come\nabout due to the rumours of sorcery and the confusion arising from the\nabandonment of villages in the area. [He goes on to suggest that additional\nresources be made available to these isolated communities so that the\nincidence of filariasis, malaria, and other vector-borne diseases could be\nreduced.]\n\nDr. Stephen Flew, SMO\nOk Tedi Mining Ltd\nFlew.Stephen.S@bhp.com.au\n--\nDr J A Ballard, Visiting Fellow\nGraduate School, Australian National University, Canberra ACT 0200, Australia\nTel +61 2 6249 5487 Fax +61 2 6249 4829\nGraduate School web page http://online.anu.edu.au/academia/graduate/\ne-mail: john.ballard@anu.edu.au\n\n[2]\nDate: Wed, 24 Dec 1997 17:11:46 +0700 (JAVT)\nFrom: zulfian <ZULFIAN@JAYAPURA.WASANTARA.NET.ID>\nWe thank you for the attention and consideration on what happen in Irian\nJaya, especially in the highland of Jayawijaya.\nThe El Nino has affected the area to cause the drought since April 1997. It\nseems that the situation has incerased the temperature in the highland. Our\nconsideration is in the area above 1,700 m (5,000 feet) from sea level,\nwhich [has seen] several changes on the ecosystem.\nWe have clinically and laboratory confirmed that cerebral malaria has been\nfound in that area (Lolat, Weawen, and Kabianggema, these three villages are\nnot in the border of Papua New Guinea). Malaria are common in Irian Jaya;\nhowever, it was amazing because the disease had never been found [at that\naltitude]. A recent investigation by a team of entomologists,\nepidemiologists, and clinical laboratorians found that the _Anopheles\npunctulatus_ [?] has climbed up to higher altitude.\nBlood smear examination from 52 [people in the?] high prevalence area had\nalso confirmed that plasmodium infections were responsible for the\nmysterious disease. The parasite rate had been found 31.9% - 98.21% among\nthe suffering patients.\nThese villages listed below are endemic [for] malaria and are on the border\nof Papua New Guinea :\n\nVillage Parasite rate\nPepera 98.21%\nBulangkop 81.71%\nMimin 54.17%\nKabiding 31.90%\nOknagul 70.00%\nCurrently, the rate has decreased to 2% after a radical mass treatment with\nchloroquine.\nAn investigation is being conducted at village Okyop (a village close to\nYapsie of Papua New Guinea), since it was reported that same problem arise\nthere. We hope that the laboratory result (blood smear) can be confirmed\nwithin a week.\n--\nZulfian Muslim, MD\nHead, District Health Office\nDistrict of Jayawijaya\nIrian Jaya, Indonesia\n[So, we have a situation in which many serious infectious diseases are\nendemic and the current hypotheses are (1) there is no excess morbidity or\nmortality but (2) primitive beliefs are driving rumors of such illnesses and\ndeaths and (3) there is cerebral malaria in the area but that it is being\ntreated properly and successfully. We likely will not get to a diagnostic\nresolution of this situation. - Mod.CHC]\n...............................chc/es\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. I would suspect underlying virus infection\nespecially if a consistent bacterial pathogen is not isolated from multiple\nsnakes. Histopathologic lesions are not always pathognomonic for\nparamyxovirus infection in snakes but a pathologist familiar with snake\nhistopathology should recognize suspicious lesions. Evaluation of organs\nother than the lung can be especially revealing (pancreas, liver). In some\ncases, pancreatic changes are more specific for this viral disease than\npulmonary changes. Isolation of this virus can be difficult and is best\nattempted by those labs with reptile virology experience.\nTwo that we have worked with successfully are the University of Florida,\nCollege of Veterinary Medicine (Dr. Elliott Jacobson) and the University of\nTennessee, College of Veterinary Medicine (Dr. Melissa Kennedy).\n--\nRebecca Papendick, DVM\nAssociate Pathologist\nZoological Society of San Diego\nSan Diego, CA, USA\ne-mail: rpapendick@sandiegozoo.org\n\n[2\nDate: Tue, 2 Dec 1997 11:07:10 -0600\nFrom: Rene Alvarez <ralvarez@icarus.sfbr.org>\n\nAn e-mail was sent to me concerning the recent outbreak of pneumonia which\nhas been causing a high morbidity and mortality in snakes at the Dallas\nZoo. I understand that attempts for bacterial isolates have been somewhat\nunsuccessful.\nI have been working for the past 2 years on a reovirus isolated from the\nCNS of a rattlesnake suffering from neurological dysfunctions, which was\nresponsible for about 50% morbidity in a breeding colony in Germany. Even\nthough the clinical symptoms described in your letter are not related a CNS\ninfection, they are classical reovirus-like symptoms. A group in Germany\nhas isolated 7 additional reovirus isolates from reptiles including two\nfrom pythons. I am in the process of obtaining these isolates to further\ncharacterize them with respect to other reoviruses including the\nrattlesnake reovirus. The rattlesnake reovirus grows well in Vero Cells\n(African green monkey kidney cells, ATCC), so perhaps you may want to\nattempt to isolate virus in this cell line. I will be more than glad to\nassist you in anyway possible.\n--\nRene Alvarez\nSouthwest Foundation for Biomedical Research\nSan Antonio, TX\ne-mail: ralvarez@icarus.sfbr.org\n[Several posts were forwarded to Dr. Gamble but I thought these two would\nbe of interest to our subscribers. - Mod.PC\n.....................................pc/es\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. It is most likely that those animals are occasionally in\ncontact with free ranging cattle which feed on the same alpine pasture\nland. Although most herpesviruses are know to have a very narrow host\nrange, an infection with a bovine herpesvirus (either BHV-4 or BHV-5) might\nhave caused the encephalitis. Another possible agent to cause the described\nsymptoms would be Borna virus, but the epidemiological data (seven affected\nanimals within two weeks) argues against it.\n--\nDr. Martin Pfeffer\nInstitute for Medical Microbiology,\nInfectious and Epidemic Diseases\nVeterinary Faculty\nLudwig-Maximilians University\nVeterinaerstr. 13\nD-80539 Munich\nGermany\nphone: +49-89-2180-2593\nFax.: +49-89-2180-2597\ne-mail: Martin.Pfeffer@micro.vetmed.uni-muenchen.de\n......................................pc/es\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. I believe we should consider\nencephalitis caused by tick-borne encephalitis (TBE) virus. Of course, the\ntypical habitat for chamois does not correspond to that of Ixodes ricinus,\nthe main vector of TBE. Moreover, TBE infection has not been associated\nwith symptoms in wild ungulates.\nCertain populations of chamois, however, occur in typical I. ricinus\nhabitats, mainly due to disturbance by human \"outdoor activities\" (hiking,\nhang-gliding etc.). Such animals appear to be more heavily infested by I.\nricinus ticks, than are roe deer, taken nearby.\nMy own studies take me to a sub-alpine site in eastern Switzerland where\nTBE transmission is frequent, and where chamois appear to be the main local\nhost for adult I. ricinus, rather than roe deer. Chamois avoid human\ncontact more than do roe deer. Chamois-dependent tick populations\ntherefore may tend not to come in contact with people, which could explain\nwhy people acquire TBE infection so sporadically in such areas.\nAlthough chamois have never been implicated as reservoir hosts for TBE\nvirus, and the involvement of ibex suggests an altitude not suitable for\nticks, the reference to a neurological disease of ungulates \"in a remote\narea\" might suggest this agent.\n[Thanks very much for the response. I am a little surprised we didn't get\nmore comments because the request was cross posted on Epivet-L, a listserve\nfor veterinary epidemiologists. FYI, Chamois is _Rupicapra rupicapra_, a\nsmall goatlike antelope found in the high mountainous areas of Europe and\nthe Caucasus. Chamois was at one time used to some extent in bookbinding,\nbut is now most famous for being a soft, pliable leather cloth made from\nthe skin of the chamois. Ibex is similar ruminant found in Europe and\nelsewhere. - Mod. pc\n--\nIvo Foppa,\nDepts. of Epidemiology and Immunology & Infectious Diseases\nHarvard School of Public Health, Boston\n.....................................pc/es\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Since September 15, hunters\nobserved at least seven animals suffering from a progressive palsy\naffecting first the posterior limbs and leading to death in about a week.\nAutopsies performed until now failed to reveal any etiology but a viral\nencephalitis is suspected by veterinarians who are still working on this\nproblem.\nThe question has been asked of a possible transmission to humans (it is the\nhunting season) or domestic animals (cattle might be in contact with\naffected wild animals).
